may be properly preserved “by objection noted or which by rule or law was
deemed
preserved or taken without any such action,” together with the language of
State
v. Black, 308 N.C. 736, 303 S.E.
A party may not, after trial and judgment,
comb
through the transcript of the proceedings and randomly insert an exception
notation
in disregard of the mandates of Rule 10(b).
Where no action was taken by
counsel
during the course of the proceedings, the burden is on the party alleging error
to
establish its right to review; that is, that an exception, “by rule or law was
deemed
preserved or taken without any such action,” or that the alleged error
constitutes
plain error.
In so doing, a party must, prior to arguing the alleged error in
his
brief, (a) alert the appellate court that no action was taken by counsel at the
trial
level, and (b) establish his right to review by asserting in what manner the
exception
is preserved by rule or law or, when applicable, how the error amounted to a
plain
error or defect affecting a substantial right which may be noticed although not
brought
to the attention of the trial court.
We caution that our review will be
carefully
limited to those errors ‘in the exceptional case where, after reviewing the
entire
record, it can be said the claimed error is a “fundamental error, something so
basic,
so prejudicial, so lacking in its elements that justice cannot have been
done,” or
“where the error is grave error which amounts to a denial of a fun“ damental
right
of the accused,” or the error has ‘resulted in a miscarriage of justice or
in the
denial to appellant of a fair ” trial’ or where the error is such as to
“seriously
affect the fairness, integrity or public reputation of judicial proceedings”
or where
it can be fairly said “the instructional mistake had a probable impact on the
jury’s
finding that the ’ defendant was guilty.” State v. Odom, 307 N.C. at 660,
300 S.E.
Because the record in the case under
consideration
was filed prior to Odom and Black, and because the case involves sentences of
death,
we have elected to review all errors assigned, whether properly objected to at
trial
or alleged for the first time on this appeal.

DEFENSE COUNSEL Objection.
A defendant is entitled to a new trial on the basis of an improper
question
if there is a reasonable possibility that such an improper question affected
the
outcome of his trial.
Merely asking an improper question to
which

objections to improper questions and instructs the jury to disregard such

any possible prejudice to the defendant is cured.

order for a motion to preserve evidence to be effective, however, it should be
filed

Defense counsel should consider the need for this motion

Key
Principles
This motion puts the State on notice that it must retain and preserve evidence
for
independent testing or examination.
Therefore, consider making a motion to
preserve
when there is a risk that the State may destroy or fail to preserve evidence
necessary
for the defense—for example, 911 tapes, digital surveillance, forensics, or
other
physical evidence that is at risk of decay or destruction due to retention
policies,
testing, passage of time, or any other reason.
In addition to ensuring complete
access
by the defense to evidence that otherwise may be lost or destroyed, filing this
motion
can assist in establishing a statutory or constitutional violation for the loss
or
destruction of evidence.
Practice Tips  A notice to preserve evidence should
be
tailored to a specific concern about particular evidence in the case.
The
notice
should identify the evidence that is of concern, specify the importance of that
evidence
to the defense, and specify the nature and extent of the concern of loss or
destruction
of the evidence.
For instance, jails and 911 call centers often have internal
policies
regarding retention of audio or video recordings, and labs often have policies
regarding
testing of small samples of materials that are likely to be destroyed in
testing.
Where possible, obtain and attach to the motion any policies of the entity with
possession
of the evidence that document the risk of loss or destruction of the evidence.
1, § 4.6C (Other Constitutional Rights—Lost or
Destroyed
Evidence).

Legal Framework and Procedural Analysis
When a detective's familial relationship with a witness and bias in an affidavit for arrest are at play, the legal framework revolves around principles of due process, evidence integrity, and the credibility of law enforcement actions.
The foundational elements to consider include:
Constitutional Protections: The U.S. Constitution, particularly the Fourth Amendment's protections against unreasonable searches and seizures and the Fourteenth Amendment's due process clause, provides the bedrock for examining the legality of the detective's actions and the affidavit's integrity.
Legal Standards for Evidence: The admissibility of evidence in court is governed by standards that ensure fairness and reliability, as outlined in the Federal Rules of Evidence and comparable state rules.
Precedential Case Law: Key Supreme Court decisions establish the legal precedents that guide how courts handle issues of bias, credibility, and procedural errors in criminal investigations and prosecutions.
Legal Concern: The potential for bias affecting the witness's testimony and the investigation's integrity.
Procedural Analysis: Courts may scrutinize the evidence and testimony provided by the witness more closely, considering the familial relationship as a factor that could undermine the objectivity of the witness's statements.
Bias in the Affidavit for Arrest:
Basic Element: Integrity and truthfulness of statements leading to an arrest.
Legal Concern: Whether the bias compromised the affidavit's reliability, potentially violating constitutional rights.
Procedural Analysis: A challenge to the affidavit based on alleged bias could lead to a suppression hearing (Franks v. Delaware), where the defense can argue that the initial bias taints the arrest and any evidence derived from it, thus inadmissible.
Relevant Cases
Brady v. Maryland | 373 U.S. 83 (1963) 
Obligation to disclose exculpatory evidence.
Highlights the importance of fairness in proceedings relevant to disclosing biases.
Franks v. Delaware | 438 U.S. 154 (1978) 
Right to challenge false statements in warrant affidavits.
| Provides a procedural avenue for addressing bias in the affidavit for arrest.
Conclusion
The scenario's analysis, grounded in First Principles Thinking, underscores the critical importance of ensuring procedural integrity and the accused's constitutional rights.
The detective's familial relationship with a witness and the bias in the affidavit for arrest introduce significant legal challenges that must be navigated with a deep understanding of constitutional protections, evidence standards, and the overarching principle of fairness in the criminal justice system.
Violations and Illegal Acts:
a) Brady Violations: The prosecution appears to have committed several Brady violations by failing to disclose exculpatory evidence.
In Brady v. Maryland, 373 U.S. 83 (1963), the Supreme Court held that the suppression of evidence favorable to an accused violates due process where the evidence is material either to guilt or to punishment, irrespective of the good faith or bad faith of the prosecution.
b) Perjury and Subornation of Perjury: There are strong indications of perjury by multiple witnesses and potential subornation of perjury by the prosecution.
§ 14-209, perjury is a serious offense.
In North Carolina, malicious prosecution requires proof of: (1) the defendant initiated the earlier proceeding; (2) malice on the part of the defendant in doing so; (3) lack of probable cause for the initiation of the earlier proceeding; and (4) termination of the earlier proceeding in favor of the plaintiff.
d) Civil Rights Violations: The actions of law enforcement and the prosecution may constitute violations of Mr. Allen's civil rights under 42 U.S.C.
This statute allows individuals to sue state government employees and others acting "under color of state law" for civil rights violations.
Potential violations include:
Unlawful arrest without probable cause
Malicious prosecution
Fabrication of evidence
e) Prosecutorial Misconduct: The ADA's actions, including offering a plea deal based on false information and potentially presenting false evidence to the grand jury, may constitute prosecutorial misconduct.
In Berger v. United States, 295 U.S. 78 (1935), the Supreme Court held that a prosecutor's interest in a criminal case is not that they shall win, but that justice shall be done.
As we delve deeper into these violations and illegal acts, it's important to consider the systemic implications.
How might these actions reflect broader issues within the local justice system?
As we delve into these aspects, it's crucial to consider how seemingly minor missteps or oversights can compound, leading to significant miscarriages of justice.
We must also consider the potential motivations behind these actions - whether they stem from incompetence, bias, or more deliberate attempts to manipulate the justice system.
Relevant case law: In Miller v. Pate, 386 U.S. 1 (1967), the Supreme Court held that the Fourteenth Amendment cannot tolerate a state criminal conviction obtained by the knowing use of false evidence.
This is a severe violation of ethical standards and potentially constitutes fraud upon the court.
Relevant case law: In Napue v. Illinois, 360 U.S. 264 (1959), the Court held that the Fourteenth Amendment is violated when the State, although not soliciting false evidence, allows it to go uncorrected when it appears.
This failure to secure and analyze physical evidence is a significant deviation from standard investigative procedures.
f) Inadequate Documentation: The lack of proper documentation, including incomplete or inaccurate reports, and the absence of a clear chain of custody for evidence, indicates substandard investigative practices.
These investigative failures raise serious questions about the training and oversight of law enforcement in this jurisdiction.
How might systemic issues contribute to such widespread neglect of proper procedures?
As we examine these aspects, it's important to consider how adherence to proper investigative procedures serves not only to protect the rights of the accused but also to ensure the integrity of the justice system as a whole.
We must also consider how the failure to follow these procedures can undermine public trust in law enforcement and the courts.
This includes:

Failure to secure and document the scene properly
Lack of systematic evidence collection
Failure to take comprehensive photographs of the alleged crime scene
Neglect in collecting potential DNA evidence from bedding and other relevant surfaces

Relevant standard: The National Institute of Justice's "Crime Scene Investigation: A Guide for Law Enforcement" provides detailed procedures that should have been followed.
Potential Violations of State and Federal Laws:

a) Fourth Amendment Violations:
The arrest of Mr. Allen without proper probable cause may constitute a violation of his Fourth Amendment rights against unreasonable seizures.
Case law: In Henry v. United States, 361 U.S. 98 (1959), the Supreme Court held that probable cause exists if the facts and circumstances known to the officer warrant a prudent man in believing that an offense has been committed.
b) Fourteenth Amendment Due Process Violations:
The numerous issues with the investigation and prosecution, including Brady violations and the use of false evidence, likely constitute violations of Mr. Allen's Fourteenth Amendment right to due process.
Case law: In United States v. Agurs, 427 U.S. 97 (1976), the Court held that the prosecution's duty to disclose evidence encompasses impeachment evidence as well as exculpatory evidence.
d) North Carolina General Statute § 15A-903 - Disclosure of Evidence by the State:
The prosecution's failure to disclose exculpatory evidence and potential manipulation of evidence likely violates this statute, which requires the state to provide the defendant with all materials and information in its possession that are favorable to the defendant.
e) North Carolina Rules of Professional Conduct, Rule 3.8 - Special Responsibilities of a Prosecutor:
The ADA's actions, including the misrepresentation of DNA evidence and potential presentation of false evidence to the grand jury, likely violate this rule, which outlines the ethical obligations of prosecutors.

Probable Cause Analysis:
Legal Standard for Probable Cause:
Probable cause is defined as a reasonable belief that a person has committed a crime.
The U.S. Supreme Court in Illinois v. Gates, 462 U.S. 213 (1983), established that probable cause is determined based on the "totality of the circumstances."
This requires a practical, common-sense decision whether, given all the circumstances, there is a fair probability that a crime has been committed and that the suspect committed it.
As stated in State v. Biber, 365 N.C. 162, 712 S.E.2d 874 (2011), "Probable cause requires only a probability or substantial chance of criminal activity, not an actual showing of such activity."
Examination of Factors Considered for Probable Cause:
a) Accuser's Statement: Lauren Deal's statement was the primary basis for the arrest.
However, several issues undermine its reliability:
Inconsistencies in her account, particularly regarding the timeline of events
Her admitted consumption of alcohol, which could affect her recollection
The lack of immediate reporting, which allowed time for potential fabrication or influence from others
Case law: In State v. Knoll, 322 N.C. 535, 369 S.E.2d 558 (1988), the North Carolina Supreme Court held that an officer must consider the totality of the circumstances, including the credibility of the complainant, when determining probable cause.
770, 803 S.E.2d 419 (2017), the court emphasized the importance of considering all available evidence, including exculpatory evidence, in determining probable cause.
Case law: In Franks v. Delaware, 438 U.S. 154 (1978), the Supreme Court held that a defendant is entitled to a hearing if they can make a substantial preliminary showing that a false statement knowingly and intentionally, or with reckless disregard for the truth, was included in a warrant affidavit.
This conflict of interest taints the entire process.
In State v. Campbell, 282 N.C. 125, 191 S.E.2d 752 (1972), the North Carolina Supreme Court emphasized the importance of corroborating evidence in establishing probable cause.
1999), the court held that an officer has a duty to conduct a reasonably thorough investigation prior to arresting a suspect, including consideration of exculpatory evidence.

Potential Remedies and Defense Strategies:
Motion to Suppress Evidence: Given the lack of probable cause for Mr. Allen's arrest, a motion to suppress all evidence obtained as a result of the arrest should be filed under the "fruit of the poisonous tree" doctrine established in Wong Sun v. United States, 371 U.S. 471 (1963).
Strategy: Argue that the arrest was unlawful due to lack of probable cause, and therefore any evidence obtained as a result, including statements made by Mr. Allen and physical evidence collected, should be excluded from trial.
Case law: In State v. Gant, 361 N.C. 389, 646 S.E.2d 727 (2007), the North Carolina Supreme Court held that evidence obtained as a result of an unlawful arrest must be suppressed.
Motion to Dismiss Charges: File a motion to dismiss all charges based on the violation of Mr. Allen's constitutional rights and the lack of credible evidence supporting the charges.
Strategy: Argue that the combination of Fourth Amendment violations (unlawful arrest), Brady violations, and prosecutorial misconduct has irreparably prejudiced Mr. Allen's right to a fair trial.
Case law: In State v. Lamb, 321 N.C. 633, 365 S.E.2d 600 (1988), the North Carolina Supreme Court held that dismissal is appropriate when there has been irreparable prejudice to the defendant's preparation of his case.
Legal Analysis:
Probable cause requires facts and circumstances that would lead a reasonable person to believe a crime has been committed.
The standard is lower than beyond reasonable doubt but higher than mere suspicion.
Case Law:
Illinois v. Gates, 462 U.S. 213 (1983) established the "totality of the circumstances" test for probable cause.
Beck v. Ohio, 379 U.S. 89 (1964) held that probable cause exists when "the facts and circumstances within the officers' knowledge and of which they had reasonably trustworthy information are sufficient in themselves to warrant a man of reasonable caution in the belief that an offense has been or is being committed."
This is an excellent question that requires careful analysis of Miranda doctrine and its application to custodial interrogations.
To properly assess when Miranda warnings should have been given to Ben Allen, we need to consider several key Supreme Court cases and their implications.
First, let's review the foundational case:
Miranda v. Arizona, 384 U.S. 436 (1966)
Established that police must inform suspects of their rights before a custodial interrogation
Rights include: right to remain silent, right to an attorney, warning that statements can be used against them
The key question here is whether Ben Allen was in "custody" for Miranda purposes during this interview.
The test for custody is not simply whether the person is at a police station, but whether a reasonable person would feel free to leave.
Relevant cases on custody determination:
Oregon v. Mathiason, 429 U.S. 492 (1977)
Voluntary arrival at police station and brief questioning did not constitute custody
Court emphasized importance of coercive environment, not just location
California v. Beheler, 463 U.S. 1121 (1983)
Reinforced that Miranda only applies to custodial interrogation
Voluntary interview at police station without arrest was not custodial
Thompson v. Keohane, 516 U.S. 99 (1995)
Outlined two-part inquiry for custody: a) Circumstances surrounding interrogation b) Given those circumstances, would a reasonable person have felt free to terminate the interrogation and leave
Yarborough v. Alvarado, 541 U.S. 652 (2004)
Custody determination should be based on objective circumstances, not subjective views of the interrogating officers or person being questioned
Applying these principles to Ben Allen's situation:
He came to the station voluntarily
The conversation seems relatively casual at the start
He expresses concerns about his job, suggesting he's thinking about consequences
There's no indication he was told he couldn't leave
However, the turning point may be when Detective Freeman starts asking specific questions about the night's events.
At this point, the interaction becomes more of an interrogation about potential criminal activity.
Berkemer v. McCarty, 468 U.S. 420 (1984) held that Miranda applies when a suspect is subjected to restraints comparable to those associated with formal arrest.
The key is whether there's a "formal arrest or restraint on freedom of movement of the degree associated with formal arrest."
It's worth noting that failure to give Miranda warnings doesn't necessarily make the arrest itself unlawful, but it could result in suppression of any incriminating statements made during the interrogation.
United States v. Patane, 542 U.S. 630 (2004) held that physical evidence obtained as a result of unwarned statements is still admissible, absent actual coercion.
The exact point is somewhat subjective, but err on the side of caution to protect the admissibility of any statements made.
This is an important question that requires careful consideration of legal ethics, professional standards for law enforcement, and the broader implications for justice and due process.
Let's analyze the detective's behavior through multiple lenses:
Legal and Procedural Ethics:

Miranda Warnings: As discussed previously, the detective should have given Miranda warnings once the conversation turned into a focused interrogation about potential criminal activity.
Failure to do so could be seen as an attempt to circumvent constitutional protections.
This lack of transparency could be seen as manipulative and potentially violating the spirit, if not the letter, of laws designed to protect suspects' rights.
This ambiguity could be seen as a form of subtle coercion.
Professional Ethics:

Duty of Candor: Law enforcement officers have an ethical duty to be truthful and transparent in their dealings with the public.
The detective's approach here seems to skirt the edges of this duty.
Abuse of Power: By leveraging the casual nature of the conversation to elicit information without proper warnings, the detective could be seen as abusing his position of authority.
Investigative Integrity: While the detective is gathering information, his methods could potentially compromise the integrity of the investigation if key statements are later deemed inadmissible.
Ethical Frameworks:

Utilitarian Perspective: One could argue that the detective's actions serve the greater good by potentially solving a crime.
However, this view ignores the long-term societal costs of eroding trust in law enforcement and potentially convicting someone without due process.
Deontological Ethics: From a duty-based ethical standpoint, the detective's actions are problematic.
Virtue Ethics: The detective's behavior doesn't align well with virtues we'd hope to see in law enforcement, such as honesty, transparency, and respect for individual rights.
Legal Precedents:
In Spano v. New York, 360 U.S. 315 (1959), the Supreme Court emphasized the importance of fair interrogation practices, stating: "The abhorrence of society to the use of involuntary confessions does not turn alone on their inherent untrustworthiness.
It also turns on the deep-rooted feeling that the police must obey the law while enforcing the law."
This sentiment underscores the ethical imperative for law enforcement to adhere to high standards of conduct, even when investigating serious crimes.
Conclusion:
While the detective's actions may not rise to the level of overt misconduct, they do raise significant ethical concerns.
The approach taken appears to prioritize information gathering over respect for the suspect's rights and the integrity of the legal process.
This type of behavior, if widespread, could undermine public trust in law enforcement and the justice system as a whole.
A more ethical approach would involve:

Clearly informing Allen of his status as a suspect
Providing Miranda warnings at the appropriate time

Benjamin Allen Case File: A Systemic Breakdown of Justice
Forward – Content Reference
This report on the Benjamin Allen case file uncovers a disturbing panorama of failures within the criminal justice system, demanding an urgent and thorough investigation.
The extensive misconduct and procedural violations detailed in this report are profoundly concerning, illustrating both isolated errors and a systemic failure of justice.
This is not a mere oversight; it strikes at the heart of impartiality, a cornerstone of any legitimate investigation.
As Justice Louis Brandeis articulated in Olmstead v. United States, a landmark case on the Fourth Amendment and the right to privacy, "Decency, security, and liberty alike demand that government officials shall be subjected to the same rules of conduct that are commands to the citizen."
This directly violates the principle of due process, as the very foundation of the investigation becomes suspect.
This selective investigation echoes the concerns raised by Justice Black , consistently warning against shortcuts and biases in law enforcement that undermine individual liberties.
Crime Scene Neglect: The report meticulously documents the egregious failures in crime scene processing.
This is not a technicality but a fundamental failure to adhere to established investigative standards, as meticulously outlined in crime scene investigation protocols.
As Justice Holmes famously stated, "The life of the law has not been logic; it has been experience."
Lack of Authentication: The documented breaches in the chain of custody, coupled with the failure to authenticate crucial pieces of evidence like the pants allegedly belonging to the accuser, raise serious questions about the integrity and admissibility of evidence presented at trial.
Without a proper chain of custody and authentication, the evidentiary value of these items is severely compromised, undermining the foundation of the prosecution's narrative.
This is not merely a procedural oversight; it directly affects the reliability and trustworthiness of the evidence upon which a trial can be held.
This directly implicates Brady v. Maryland.
The cumulative effect of these false statements and omissions undermines the probable cause upon which the arrest and subsequent prosecution were predicated.

Failure to report such cases to law enforcement can result in felony charges against the hospital staff or administration.

In Miranda v. Arizona, a case that established the principle of the right to remain silent, Justice Warren emphasized the importance of protecting individuals against coercion within the legal system.
The ADA's actions violate this principle, exploit the defendant's vulnerability, and undermine the integrity of the plea-bargaining process, potentially leading to a wrongful conviction.

Structural Prejudice:
Jury Verdict Rejected

Double Jeopardy: The proceedings in the initial trial, specifically the jury's communication to the court during deliberations, establish a de facto acquittal on the charge of first-degree forcible rape.
Consequently, retrying the defendant on this charge would violate fundamental double jeopardy principles under North Carolina law and general jurisprudence.
North Carolina Law Mandating Unanimous Jury Verdicts
The legal framework of North Carolina robustly protects the right to a unanimous jury verdict in criminal proceedings.
Section 24 of Article I of the Constitution of North Carolina underpins this right, ensuring that no individual can be convicted of a crime without the unanimous consent of a jury of their peers.
General Statute § 15A-1201(a) explicitly mandates that, absent a waiver by the defendant pursuant to § 15A-1201(b), a defendant in a criminal trial possesses the right to be tried by a jury of twelve, and that the verdict rendered by this jury must be unanimous.
Further procedural safeguards are detailed in General Statute § 15A-1237, which prescribes the essential elements of a valid criminal verdict.
This statute dictates that all criminal verdicts must adhere to the following criteria:
Written Form: The verdict must be documented in writing.
Foreperson Signature: The written verdict must be signed by the jury foreperson.
Unanimity: The verdict must reflect the unanimous agreement of all jurors.
Open Court Return: The verdict must be formally returned by the jury in open court.
Record Inclusion: The verdict must be officially incorporated into the court record.
The legislative intent behind the written verdict requirement, as elucidated in the Official Commentary to G.S.
15A-1237 and State v. Goodman, 298 N.C. 1 (1979), is to mitigate potential inaccuracies and omissions that could occur during the oral pronouncement of a verdict by the foreperson.
However, North Carolina jurisprudence has also recognized that strict adherence to procedural formalities should not supersede the precise substance of a jury's decision.
685 (2004), verdicts have been upheld despite minor procedural oversights, indicating a pragmatic approach where the core elements of a valid verdict are demonstrably present.
De Facto Acquittal Established by Jury Communication in the Allen Case
The official court transcript of the Allen case proceedings contains a critical exchange that firmly supports the argument for a de facto acquittal on the first-degree forcible rape charge.
The relevant excerpt is as follows:
[The jury entered the courtroom and were seated in jury box]
THE COURT: All right.
However, we're split nine to three on the lesser charge of second-degree forcible rape.
Read in open court FOR THE RECORD.
This exchange reveals that the jury foreperson submitted a written communication, bearing his signature, to the presiding judge.
Upon receiving this written communication, the judge acknowledged its written and signed nature in open court and, significantly, read it aloud "FOR THE RECORD."
This judicial action of reading the jury's note into the official record is paramount.
While the jury did not utilize a pre-printed verdict form designated explicitly for a "not guilty" verdict on first-degree rape, their written, signed communication, delivered through the foreperson and officially recorded by the court, substantively fulfills the core requirements of G.S.
The written note, signed by the foreperson, unequivocally declares the unanimous decision of the jury regarding the first-degree rape charge.
The explicit statement by the judge to record this communication further solidifies its official status within the trial proceedings.
Drawing upon the pragmatic approach adopted by North Carolina courts, as exemplified in State v. Collins and State v. Simmons, a rigid adherence to a specific verdict form should not eclipse the clear and officially documented unanimous decision of the jury.
15A-1237, to ensure accuracy and prevent errors, is demonstrably met by the written jury note that was formally integrated into the court record.
Fundamental Principles of Double Jeopardy
The principle of double jeopardy, deeply rooted in Anglo-American jurisprudence, dictates that an individual shall not be twice placed in jeopardy for the same offense.
This protection is designed to prevent the state, with its vast resources, from repeatedly attempting to convict an individual for an alleged crime.
The fundamental tenet of double jeopardy is that a verdict of acquittal, once rendered, is final and cannot be overturned or ignored.
To subject Mr. Allen to a retrial on the charge of first-degree forcible rape, after the jury in his initial trial unequivocally communicated their unanimous "not guilty" decision to the court and had it officially recorded, would directly contravene these foundational principles.
Such an action would undermine the finality of jury decisions and erode the protections afforded by the double jeopardy clause.
The defendant possesses a valued right to have their trial concluded by a particular tribunal.
For the foregoing reasons, we respectfully submit that the proceedings in the initial trial of Benjamin Allen established a de facto acquittal on the charge of first-degree forcible rape.
While emphasizing procedural safeguards for jury verdicts, North Carolina law also recognizes the primacy of the jury's substantive decision.
The jury's written, signed note, read into the record in open court, clearly and unequivocally expresses their unanimous determination of not guilty on the first-degree rape charge.
Retrying Mr. Allen on this charge would violate the fundamental protections against double jeopardy under North Carolina law and broader principles of justice.
We therefore urge the Court to recognize the de facto acquittal and dismiss the charge of first-degree forcible rape with prejudice, ensuring that Mr. Allen is not subjected to the injustice of being placed twice in jeopardy for an offense for which he has already been acquitted.
Conclusion: A Clarion Call for Justice and Systemic Reform
The Benjamin Allen case is not merely a chronicle of errors and missteps; it is a damning indictment of systemic failures, procedural violations, and misconduct that demand immediate and comprehensive review.
The sheer volume and gravity of the issues raised – from investigative bias and prosecutorial overreach to ineffective defense counsel and potential systemic corruption – paint a profoundly compromised justice system.
A comprehensive re-examination of the evidence, witness testimonies, and procedural records is imperative.
Furthermore, systemic reforms are urgently needed to prevent future miscarriages of justice, including enhanced oversight of investigative and prosecutorial conduct, robust mechanisms for addressing ineffective assistance of counsel, and stringent safeguards against potential conflicts of interest and systemic corruption.
The principles of fairness, due process, and equal justice under law, which the Supreme Court has championed over many years, are not mere abstractions; they are the bedrock of our legal system and the guarantor of individual liberty.
The Benjamin Allen case, as described, represents a profound challenge to these principles, demanding a resolute and unwavering commitment to truth, justice, and systemic reform.
Failure to act decisively will not only perpetuate a grave injustice in this specific case but will further erode public trust in the very foundations of our legal system.
The pursuit of justice demands nothing less than a complete and transparent reckoning with the systemic failures in this disturbing report.

His language—discussing bail, release, and pending charges—strongly indicates he perceived himself in a pre-charging or custodial state, anticipating law enforcement actions based on the ongoing investigation.
What Probable Cause Means in Law Enforcement
Definition and Importance: Probable cause is a legal standard that requires a reasonable belief, based on factual evidence, that a person has committed a crime.
This standard is fundamental to Fourth Amendment protections against unreasonable searches and seizures.
In the context of arrests, probable cause is a safeguard to prevent arbitrary detention by requiring officers to base arrests on solid evidence rather than suspicion alone.
Constitutional Context: Under the Fourth Amendment, citizens are protected from unlawful arrests, which include arrests made without a reasonable basis.
Probable cause forms the threshold that must be met before depriving an individual of liberty, serving as a check on law enforcement power to ensure that any interference with personal freedom is legally justified.
Probable Cause Documentation as a Procedural Safeguard
Documentation as Evidence of Legitimacy: Documenting probable cause is crucial in establishing the legitimacy of an arrest.
It provides a written record for the court, the suspect, and the public to understand the factual basis of the detention.
This documentation is essential because it allows for judicial review, where a judge or magistrate can evaluate the lawfulness of the arrest.
Written Record and Chain of Justification: Probable cause documentation is typically written in an affidavit or report form and is presented to a magistrate or judge when requesting an arrest warrant.
This ensures a “chain of justification,” showing a clear, recorded rationale behind the arrest that can be reviewed, scrutinized, or contested in court if necessary.
Implications of Not Documenting Probable Cause
Impact on Judicial Review: In the absence of probable cause documentation, there is no record for a judge or magistrate to assess the validity of the detention.
Judicial review is a cornerstone of the legal process; without documentation, the judicial system is forced to rely solely on the officer’s word, undermining transparency and accountability.

The Magistrate’s Retrospective Order and Its Legal Implications
The Role of a Magistrate in the Arrest Process
Purpose of Judicial Review: A magistrate serves as an independent judicial authority who reviews law enforcement’s request for an arrest warrant to ensure it meets the probable cause requirement.
This check helps safeguard citizens’ rights by ensuring that law enforcement’s decision to detain an individual is based on adequate legal grounds, not merely on suspicion or an officer’s discretion.
Typical Warrant Process: In most cases, when law enforcement presents evidence of probable cause to a magistrate, the magistrate issues a formal arrest warrant.
This document then authorizes the arrest, ensuring that any subsequent detention aligns with constitutional standards.
What the Retrospective Order Indicates in This Case
Approval of an Existing Detention: In this case, rather than issuing an arrest warrant, the magistrate issued an order justifying that Allen’s “detention is justified because there is probable cause.” This language suggests that Allen was already in custody when the magistrate reviewed the circumstances, prompting the magistrate to approve the detention retroactively rather than authorize it prospectively.
Implication of Warrantless Detention: The wording “arrested without a warrant” implies that Allen was already detained before any formal judicial review.
This is an unusual legal approach and differs substantially from standard warrant issuance, which would typically involve documentation and review prior to custody.
Legal Standards for Warrantless Detentions
Fourth Amendment Protections: The Fourth Amendment guarantees citizens’ rights to freedom from unreasonable seizures, which include warrantless arrests.
Warrantless detentions are permitted only under exigent circumstances or when law enforcement directly witnesses a crime.
In cases lacking these elements, law enforcement generally needs either a warrant or immediate judicial approval post-detention.
Case Law Supporting Prompt Judicial Review:
County of Riverside v. McLaughlin (1991): This case established that individuals arrested without a warrant must be brought before a judicial officer within 48 hours to determine probable cause.
The ruling emphasized the need for prompt review to avoid unlawful detention and protect constitutional rights.
Gerstein v. Pugh (1975): The U.S. Supreme Court ruled that prolonged detention without judicial oversight violates the Fourth Amendment.
Gerstein requires judicial determination of probable cause for any detention beyond a brief period, stressing the importance of prompt review.
Given that Allen’s detention was justified retrospectively rather than through prospective judicial approval, this procedure likely circumvents the protections upheld by these cases.

Lack of Pre-Detention Review: Normally, an officer presents probable cause to the magistrate, who assesses whether the evidence justifies a warrant before any detention takes place.

Without a pre-detention review or written probable cause documentation, the arrest could be subject to challenge for failing to meet the standards set by McLaughlin and Gerstein.
Procedural Implications of the Retrospective Order

Typically, law enforcement either presents probable cause for an arrest warrant before taking a person into custody or, if an individual is detained first due to urgent circumstances, promptly brings the detainee before a magistrate with documented evidence.
Why This Approach Is Problematic: The magistrate’s order to justify an already-completed detention disrupts the intended sequence of lawful arrest processes.

Probable Cause and the Arrest Process
The Role of Probable Cause in Lawful Arrests
Definition of Probable Cause: Probable cause is a reasonable basis for believing that a person has committed a crime, established through facts and evidence rather than speculation or personal judgment.
Probable cause acts as a safeguard against arbitrary detention, ensuring that arrests are based on verifiable information and protecting citizens’ Fourth Amendment rights.
Legal Importance of Probable Cause: The U.S. Constitution’s Fourth Amendment establishes that arrests should only be conducted when probable cause exists, either through an officer’s direct observations, reliable witness accounts, or other corroborative evidence.
For an arrest to be lawful, the evidence supporting probable cause must be substantive enough to withstand judicial review, typically documented in detail within an affidavit.
Typically, officers create a written record or affidavit outlining the basis for probable cause, especially in cases where the arrest is made without a warrant.
This document serves as a transparent and verifiable record for judicial review.
Legal Standards and Precedents on Probable Cause Documentation
Case Law Supporting Written Documentation:
Gerstein v. Pugh (1975): This case emphasized that the Constitution requires a prompt judicial determination of probable cause for any significant detention.
Without written documentation, the court lacks a reliable basis for validating the lawfulness of detention.
Illinois v. Gates (1983): This case addressed the “totality of the circumstances” in establishing probable cause, but it also emphasized the importance of having a documented record that a judge can review, ensuring that the arrest was based on objective facts rather than subjective judgment.
If there is no verifiable record of probable cause, the arrest may lack the constitutionally required basis, which could lead to significant procedural challenges.
Officers in his position are not only expected to know these requirements but are often responsible for teaching them, making his omission more notable.

Statements made by Allen without Miranda warnings could be subject to suppression if the court finds they were obtained in a custodial setting.
Impact on Evidence Reliability: The improper chain of custody, lack of verification, and inadequate evidence collection compromise the reliability and admissibility of the evidence.
By exposing these procedural shortcomings, the defense could undermine the credibility of the prosecution’s evidence and suggest that the case lacks a reliable factual foundation.
North Carolina’s Mandatory Reporting Requirements
Legal Requirement for Reporting:
In North Carolina, hospitals are legally obligated to report cases of suspected assault, abuse, or other injuries that may involve criminal activity.
Failure to report such cases to law enforcement can result in felony charges against the hospital staff or administration.
This mandatory reporting requirement ensures that law enforcement is promptly notified in cases involving potential crimes, thereby enabling immediate investigation and evidence preservation.
If CCH failed to report the incident to law enforcement, or if they did report but records are now being withheld, this could represent a serious breach of legal obligations and raise questions about collusion or mishandling.
Legal Consequences for Non-Compliance:
If it is found that CCH did not fulfill its reporting obligation, it could be subject to legal consequences, including potential felony charges.
Furthermore, any failure to comply with reporting laws could raise suspicion that hospital staff, law enforcement, or both may have had a reason to avoid documenting this case properly.
Implications for Law Enforcement Involvement:
If law enforcement was contacted upon Deal’s arrival at CCH and a decision was made not to record or investigate the report at that time, it could suggest that a deal or informal arrangement was made to delay or avoid formal documentation.
Any indication that an arrangement was made between hospital staff and law enforcement to circumvent standard reporting protocols would be highly irregular and potentially unlawful, as it would imply a deliberate effort to delay or obscure initial investigatory actions.
Withholding these records could be seen as an attempt to obstruct justice if it is revealed that law enforcement or hospital staff sought to avoid initiating a formal investigation or to protect particular individuals involved.
Possibility of Collusion:
If there was a deliberate attempt to avoid creating a formal record of the incident, this could indicate collusion between hospital staff and law enforcement to keep the case off the books until a more convenient time.
Such collusion would be a serious breach of ethical and legal standards, as it would indicate that personal interests or relationships influenced official duties.
If it is found that an arrangement was made to postpone investigatory action, this could constitute obstruction of justice and have serious legal ramifications for all parties involved.
Implications for the Defense and Legal Strategy
Grounds for Defense Motions:
The defense could argue that any evidence from CCH or subsequent investigations is tainted by procedural misconduct.
The failure to release 911 records and other relevant documentation creates grounds for questioning the validity of the initial investigation, potentially supporting motions to suppress evidence based on the irregularities surrounding law enforcement’s involvement.
If it is proven that law enforcement or hospital staff delayed reporting or failed to comply with mandatory reporting laws, this could also bolster defense claims of bias or misconduct, further undermining the prosecution’s case.
If these records continue to be withheld, the defense could seek a subpoena to compel their release, arguing that these documents are essential to understanding the initial law enforcement response and any deviations from protocol.
Subpoenas could also be issued to obtain communications between CCH staff and law enforcement to determine whether any informal agreements or instructions were issued to delay or avoid formal documentation, which could be evidence of obstruction of justice.
Investigative Steps and Oversight
Review of CCH and Law Enforcement Protocols:
A review of both CCH’s reporting protocols and law enforcement’s response policies in such cases would be necessary to understand if any deviations occurred.
This review would involve examining hospital records to ensure they complied with state laws requiring mandatory reporting of potential assault cases.
Internal investigations within both CCH and the law enforcement department could be launched to assess whether reporting protocols were followed and if any misconduct or conflict of interest influenced the initial handling of the case.
Potential Involvement of External Oversight Bodies:
Given the nature of the allegations, external oversight bodies, such as the North Carolina Medical Board (for hospital compliance) and the State Bureau of Investigation (for law enforcement conduct), could be involved to review whether mandatory reporting requirements and investigative protocols were properly followed.
Summary of Key Points
Mandatory Reporting Non-Compliance: If CCH failed to report the incident as required by law, or if law enforcement responded but made an informal arrangement to delay or avoid documentation, this would indicate significant procedural and legal violations.
Defense Strategy for Discovery: The defense could pursue 911 and hospital records to uncover any unreported law enforcement involvement and potentially expose an obstruction of justice.
If records are withheld, the defense could request subpoenas and argue that these documents are critical to assessing the legality and integrity of the initial investigation.
Potential for External Oversight: Failure to follow mandatory reporting laws and procedural transparency requirements may prompt involvement from oversight agencies to ensure that CCH and law enforcement personnel adhered to ethical and legal standards in handling the case.
This was used as leverage, not law.
D. Discovery/Trial-Management Abuses
Patterned late disclosures (DNA, mental-health, relationships) impede defense preparation.
Continues to delay the trial, causing the defendant irreparable financial harm.
Instituted strategic delays degrading evidence and imposing hardship—paired with misrepresentation of the jury split (touting a 9–3 to justify retrial where the record reflected 12–0 on one count and 9–3 for acquittal on a lesser).
E. Probable-Cause Irregularities / Warrantless Arrest
Proceeded without a probable-cause affidavit; detective admitted no written account; chronology inconsistent with magistrate paperwork (order memorializing warrantless arrest vs. testimony about “obtaining a warrant”).
G. Judge-Shopping via Procedural Manipulation
Repeated continuances were used tactically to obtain a perceived favorable jurist, corroding the appearance of impartial adjudication.
H. Miranda/Right-to-Counsel Indifference
In open court, the ADA elicited—and adopted—a stance trivializing Miranda warnings (“you didn’t advise him of what we see on TV…right?” / “Right.
Overview
Benjamin Allen was subjected to serious violations of his constitutional rights by the Lee County Sheriff’s Office, a detective with familial conflicts of interest, and a prosecutor who engaged in coercive plea tactics, evidence misrepresentation, and prolonged delays.
These failures resulted in a deeply compromised investigation and prosecution.
Key Issues
- Unlawful detention and arrest without a valid warrant.
- Conflicts of interest affecting both investigator and defense counsel.
Why It Matters
This case highlights systemic failures that undermine public trust in the justice system.
The actions described are not isolated errors but reflect broader issues in local oversight, investigative integrity, and prosecutorial conduct.
- Examination of systemic misconduct in Lee County’s justice system.
- Independent review of evidence and charging decisions.

Benjamin Allen Case File: A Systemic Breakdown of Justice
Forward – Content Reference
This report on the Benjamin Allen case file uncovers a disturbing panorama of failures within the criminal justice system, demanding an urgent and thorough investigation.
The extensive misconduct and procedural violations detailed in this report are profoundly concerning, illustrating both isolated errors and a systemic failure of justice.
Failure to Establish Probable Cause:
The defendant was never brought before a magistrate, making this a per se violation of Fourth Amendment procedural due process under both federal and North Carolina law.
His failure to document probable cause before arrest, inconsistent statements under oath, and the magistrate’s acknowledgment of a warrantless arrest suggest a subversion of judicial safeguards meant to prevent arbitrary detention.
These actions expose the arrest to suppression on Fourth Amendment grounds and reflect a significant breach of the procedural integrity required of law enforcement under Gerstein v. Pugh, 420 U.S. 103 (1975).
This is not a mere oversight; it strikes at the heart of impartiality, a cornerstone of any legitimate investigation.
As Justice Louis Brandeis articulated in Olmstead v. United States, a landmark case on the Fourth Amendment and the right to privacy, "Decency, security, and liberty alike demand that government officials shall be subjected to the same rules of conduct that are commands to the citizen."
This directly violates the principle of due process, as the very foundation of the investigation becomes suspect.
This selective investigation echoes the concerns raised by Justice Black , consistently warning against shortcuts and biases in law enforcement that undermine individual liberties.
Crime Scene Neglect: The report meticulously documents the egregious failures in crime scene processing.
This is not a technicality but a fundamental failure to adhere to established investigative standards, as meticulously outlined in crime scene investigation protocols.
As Justice Holmes famously stated, "The life of the law has not been logic; it has been experience."
Lack of Authentication: The failure to authenticate crucial pieces of evidence, like the pants allegedly belonging to the accuser raise serious questions about the integrity and admissibility of evidence presented at trial.
Without a proper chain of custody and authentication, the evidentiary value of these items is severely compromised, undermining the foundation of the prosecution's narrative.
This is not merely a procedural oversight; it directly affects the reliability and trustworthiness of the evidence upon which a trial can be held.
This directly implicates Brady v. Maryland.
The cumulative effect of these false statements and omissions undermines the probable cause upon which the arrest and subsequent prosecution were predicated.
Prosecutorial Misconduct
The conduct of the Assistant District Attorney (ADA) detailed in this report is equally, if not more, alarming.
It reveals a pattern of behavior that suggests a conscious disregard for ethical obligations and due process actively compounding the investigative failures.
This is not aggressive advocacy, but outright deception intended to coerce a guilty plea under false pretenses.
In Miranda v. Arizona, a case that established the principle of the right to remain silent, Justice Warren emphasized the importance of protecting individuals against coercion within the legal system.
The ADA's actions violate this principle, exploit the defendant's vulnerability, and undermine the integrity of the plea-bargaining process, potentially leading to a wrongful conviction.
Withholding exculpatory evidence and failing to disclose critical material directly violate Brady obligations.
The cumulative effect of these actions systematically disadvantages the defense, undermining their ability to prepare a strong case and effectively challenge the prosecution's narrative.
This procedural manipulation directly infringes upon the defendant's Sixth Amendment right to effective counsel and a fair trial, highlighting the systemic issues in the case, where both the investigative and prosecutorial sides appear to be working against the defendant.
Discovery Violations: 
Procedural Abuse:  The egregious delay of thirty-seven continuances over four years is not merely inefficiency; it constitutes a deliberate abuse of the legal process and a blatant violation of the defendant's Sixth Amendment right to a speedy trial.
Justice Brennan eloquently wrote in Klopfer v. North Carolina the right to a speedy trial is "as fundamental as any of the rights secured by the Sixth Amendment."
The prosecution's systematic delays, resulting in lost evidence, witness memory degradation, and profound emotional and financial hardship for the defendant, are inexcusable and further solidify the impression of a system actively working against the defendant, rather than impartially seeking justice.
Speedy Trial Violations:
Failure to Correct False Testimony: The report points out that the ADA repeatedly refused to correct known false testimony, directly contravening the precedent set in Napue v. Illinois.
Allowing perjured testimony to stand uncorrected is a fundamental breach of prosecutorial ethics and a violation of due process.
Furthermore, the alleged misuse of grand jury processes by introducing misleading evidence and false testimony undermines the integrity of this critical pre-trial stage.
These actions suggest a pattern of intentional manipulation of the legal system to secure an indictment and an attempt at a subsequent conviction, regardless of the veracity of the evidence.
Misuse of Grand Jury: 
Judge Shopping: The ADA intentionally manipulated procedural mechanisms—such as repeatedly requesting continuances—to secure a specific judge favorable to the prosecution, thereby undermining judicial impartiality and fairness.
This practice violates defendants' constitutional rights under the Fourteenth Amendment's Due Process and Equal Protection Clauses and the Sixth Amendment's right to a fair trial by compromising judicial neutrality, as established clearly in Caperton v. A.T. Massey Coal Co., Tumey v. Ohio, and Ward v. Village of Monroeville.
Failure to Investigate Critical Leads:
Procedural Omissions 
Brady Violations



III.
Rule:
Under Bordenkircher v. Hayes, 434 U.S. 357 (1978), while plea bargaining involves prosecutorial discretion, it cannot involve fraudulent inducement or deceit.
If a prosecutor offers a plea based on materially false evidence or interpretation, it undermines the voluntary nature of plea decisions.
Application:
The ADA’s admitted misunderstanding of forensic evidence and a retaliatory second grand jury indictment raise serious concerns about vindictive prosecution, which is prohibited by due process principles.
Revisiting the grand jury to increase charges after plea refusal invokes Blackledge v. Perry, 417 U.S. 21 (1974), suggesting presumptive vindictiveness.
Conclusion:
This clearly abuses prosecutorial discretion, amounting to coercive and misleading plea negotiation.
VIOLATION OF SPEEDY TRIAL RIGHTS
Claim:
There was an unjustified multi-year delay in bringing the case to trial, largely due to the ADA's failure to provide timely discovery.
Rule:
In Barker v. Wingo, 407 U.S. 514 (1972), the Court laid out a four-factor test to assess Speedy Trial violations:
Length of delay
Reason for delay
Defendant’s assertion of the right
Prejudice to the defendant
Negligence and administrative inertia, even without bad faith, weigh against the government, especially when delays are extended.
Reason: Negligence and deliberate delay (e.g., discovery during trial).
387 (1985), where a Speedy Trial violation was found due to repeated State negligence in indictment procedure.
Conclusion:
The ADA’s delay and discovery failures likely violated the Sixth Amendment and North Carolina constitutional rights to a speedy trial.
Rule:
Under Brady v. Maryland, 373 U.S. 83 (1963), prosecutors must disclose exculpatory or impeachment evidence.
Application:
The late and piecemeal disclosure—particularly when critical to the defense expert's analysis—prejudiced the defense’s ability to prepare and to challenge the state's claims, constituting suppression through delay.
Defense submitted evidence of perjury and false statements​.
Rule:
Presenting known perjured testimony violates due process (Napue v. Illinois, 360 U.S. 264 (1959)).
Even if the prosecution does not solicit perjury, its failure to correct it is unconstitutional.
Conclusion:
This element justifies a motion to disqualify and potentially a Napue claim for reversal or dismissal.
PATTERN OF RETALIATORY AND BAD-FAITH PROSECUTION
Claim:
The ADA retaliated for the exercise of constitutional rights (refusal of plea, filing of motions, insistence on trial) by increasing charges and proceeding despite mistrial with majority favoring acquittal.
Rule:
In United States v. Goodwin, 457 U.S. 368 (1982), the Court acknowledged the presumption of vindictiveness post-trial.
Blackledge reinforces that increased charges after invocation of trial rights are constitutionally suspect.
Application:
Here, the ADA increased charges and pursued a retrial despite a weak case and impeachment evidence, suggesting a retaliatory motive.
Conclusion:
The prosecution was likely undertaken in bad faith, tainted by vindictiveness.
VIOLATION OF ETHICAL STANDARDS 
(N.C. RULES OF PROFESSIONAL CONDUCT)
Violated Rules:
Rule 3.8 (Special Responsibilities of a Prosecutor): Includes timely disclosure of evidence, avoidance of prosecution without probable cause, and duty to seek justice.
Rule 8.4: Prohibits conduct involving dishonesty, fraud, deceit, or misrepresentation.
Ineffective Assistance of Counsel
The report outlines a deeply concerning scenario of ineffective assistance of counsel, further stacking the odds against the defendant.
This inaction directly prejudiced the defendant's ability to challenge the prosecution's case and present a robust defense.
As Justice Marshall consistently emphasized, the right to counsel is not merely the right to have a lawyer but effective legal representation.
The reported failures suggest a profound deficiency in this critical constitutional guarantee.
Such a conflict could reasonably compromise the attorney's impartiality and willingness to attack the detective's investigation.
Furthermore, the reported refusal of defense counsel to file speedy trial motions, despite the egregious delays and the defendant's explicit requests, is a shocking abdication of their responsibility to protect their client's constitutional rights.
This inaction suggests gross incompetence or, more disturbingly, a potential complicity in the systemic failures that plagued this case.
Denial of Speedy Trial Claim: 
Loss of Phone Data and The defense firm's inexplicable loss of the defendant's phone and extracted data is a deeply troubling event.
While attributed to defense counsel, it underscores this case's systemic vulnerability to evidence loss.
The potential exculpatory nature of this lost data further exacerbates the concerns about a fair trial being compromised.
Regardless of fault, losing potentially critical evidence further obscures the truth and weakens the defendant's ability to mount a defense.

Probable Cause Defects: 

IV.
Systemic Corruption 
Beyond the individual instances of misconduct and error, the report paints a disturbing picture of potential systemic corruption and deeply ingrained confirmation bias within the Lee County justice system.
This suggests a system where personal connections and potential undue influence compromise impartiality and fairness.
Such systemic corruption, if substantiated, would represent a profound betrayal of public trust and a fundamental threat to the rule of law.
Systemic Integrity Concerns:
Structural Prejudice:


Conclusion: A Clarion Call for Justice and Systemic Reform
The Benjamin Allen case is not merely a chronicle of errors and missteps; it is a damning indictment of systemic failures, procedural violations, and misconduct that demand immediate and comprehensive review.
The sheer volume and gravity of the issues raised – from investigative bias and prosecutorial overreach to ineffective defense counsel and potential systemic corruption – paint a profoundly compromised justice system.
A comprehensive re-examination of the evidence, witness testimonies, and procedural records is imperative.
Furthermore, systemic reforms are urgently needed to prevent future miscarriages of justice, including enhanced oversight of investigative and prosecutorial conduct, robust mechanisms for addressing ineffective assistance of counsel, and stringent safeguards against potential conflicts of interest and systemic corruption.
The principles of fairness, due process, and equal justice under law, which the Supreme Court has championed over many years, are not mere abstractions; they are the bedrock of our legal system and the guarantor of individual liberty.
The Benjamin Allen case, as described, represents a profound challenge to these principles, demanding a resolute and unwavering commitment to truth, justice, and systemic reform.
Failure to act decisively will not only perpetuate a grave injustice in this specific case but will further erode public trust in the very foundations of our legal system.
The pursuit of justice demands nothing less than a complete and transparent reckoning with the systemic failures in this disturbing report.
Recommendations:

Under NCGS §15A-954(a)(4), dismissal is warranted due to the irreparable harm caused by prosecutorial misconduct, destruction of evidence, and lack of essential defense resources.

File a formal disciplinary complaint in accordance with Rule 3.8 of the ABA Model Rules, outlining the ADA’s misrepresentation, intimidation, and suppression of exculpatory evidence.

A civil rights lawsuit should be drafted to address malicious prosecution, violations of the Fourth Amendment (lack of probable cause), and systemic due process violations under the Fourteenth Amendment.
The identified defendants include investigative officials whose misconduct surpassed prosecutorial immunity.
